actually only four of the six coordinates that appear the quadrants from one to two the quadrants from two to three from three to four and from four to one only those four quadrances appear okay so what you do is you first calculate the quadruple quad function of these four things and this is sort of an ingredient in here okay so when you're calculating this you can calculate this along the way then you have these numbers m and p you just write down this quadratic equation and the quadra of the cyclic quadrilateral is a solution to this quadratic equation so in general if you're given these four quadrances you should expect there to be well depends on the quadratic equation two solutions or maybe in some special situations one solution or maybe in some situations no solution at all the two solutions correspond to basically the brahmagupta possibility and the robin's possibility having a convex quadrilateral with given quadrances or maybe having a non-convex quadrilateral both possibilities are contained in this quadratic equation and this general principle that if we we have these two possibilities convex or non-convex instead of trying to separate them and identify them both we should think about them together right and this quadratic equation does that for us all right so this is a reformulation of the brahmagupta and robin's formulas but it's also more than that okay it's more than just a restatement because it's actually a correct statement because it's actually a statement that has ingredients which are all logically defined we are not relying on a prior theory of real numbers to understand various distances or lengths that appear because there are no distances or lengths in here so i think one can fairly say that this is really the first time that these results have been stated in a logically correct fashion that's a very big statement which has lots of consequences for other things too we'll see also later on that this formulation allows us in fact to generalize these formulas in in many different directions okay so this is a very good formulation for a number of different reasons which we'll talk about in subsequent videos but so i offer you this theorem as strong evidence of the utility of thinking rationally and logically and clearly about the foundations of mathematics and geometry.

# 134 - Absolute versus relative measurements in geometry ｜ Rational Geometry Math Foundations 134 [_c5v14ZIUO0].en-en

Kind: captions Language: en [Music] good day I'm Norman Wildberger today we're going to talk about the difference between absolute and relative measurement particularly in Geometry we have a chance to go back to some of the thinking of the ancient Greeks but also to Forefront of moving ahead with geometry today okay so the Notions of absolute measurements and relative measurements are in fact very familiar to us from everyday life and this is a well-known Concept in everyday life an absolute measurement is something like fred is 190 cm tall involves a particular number and a certain amount of units measuring some particular quantity and there's only one object involved this case Fred we're just measuring Fred in an absolute sense or the book weighs 1 kilog we're just talking about one book and making a measurement about its weight or light takes 8 minutes to get from the Sun to the Earth that's involving two objects but we're really only measuring one quantity now a relative measurement is where we have two kinds of similar things and we're comparing them but we're not necessarily invoking any units so for example Fred is twice as tall as Hilda this is independent of of whether we're measuring in centimeters or in inches feet meters or some other system just the fact that Fred is twice as tall as Hilda a relative measurement comparing Fred with Hilda or the book weighs onethird of the case this statement doesn't tell us how much the book in the case weighs just tells us the relationship between the two and an octave represents a frequency ratio of 2: one you go from La La that's an OCT I and the pythagoreans realize that's related to the ratio of 2:1 frequencies so absolute measurements typically involve units we have some choice of measuring yard stick for whatever we're measuring whether it be length or weight or something else while relative measurements are generally pure ratios or proportions they're in some sense unitless quantities because the units whatever we're using sort of cancel out so this twice here is a unitless thing the 1/3 is unitless the two to one is unitless so these are very familiar kind of distinctions between different ways of thinking about measuring in our Ordinary World but they have a lot of applications to mathematics too and that's what we're going to be interested in today the ancient Greeks in particular the pythagoreans held proportions in high regard for them the world was governed by ratios perhaps more than by numbers it was the ratios of things that were often the most interesting things for them in have a geometry with a metrical structure and we're moving things in such a way that the metrical structure is essentially unchanged now there's an important additional aspect of these translations construct the the curvature geometrically by taking some nearby points B and C to the point that we're interested in still on the curve and then we can say join the lines from a to c and a to B and
